'We came to the land... and it is flowing with milk and honey, and this is its fruit. However, the people are strong... and the cities...'" This is also implied in Rashi there, that this was the "bit of truth" they said so that the lie they said at the end would be sustained.

4. The explanation for all this is as follows:

It is known that among the mitzvos of the Torah, there are mitzvos whose essence is the action, the performance of the mitzvah, such as the mitzvah of Tefillin of the hand, where the mitzvah is to put on Tefillin. And there are mitzvos whose essence is the result and the effect, such as Tefillin of the head, where the essence of the mitzvah is that they be on the head, as it is written, "And they shall be as frontlets between your eyes".

Similarly, there is room to investigate the essence of the mission, "Be courageous and take of the fruit of the land": was the primary intent of the mission only the effect and the result—that they would return and show the fruits to the Children of Israel—or was there an obligation on the act of taking itself? Since Moshe emphasized in his words, "Be courageous and take of the fruit of the land," the act of taking, it follows that the act of the person (the agent), the taking of the fruits and carrying them, is an essential part of the mission.

One could say (at least with some strain) that the view of the Shas is like the first approach, that their mission was the effect and the result. Therefore, we do not care about the act of carrying the fruits, for there is no obligation of carrying, only that the fruits be brought (in any way) to Moshe and Israel.

Based on this, the two reasons in the Shas for why Joshua and Caleb did not carry fruits (and were not concerned with fulfilling Moshe’s command, "Be courageous and take of the fruit of the land") are understood: "because they were important... because they were not part of that counsel." This is because

## 51

in any case, Moshe’s mission was fulfilled by others, and the command did not fall upon each one individually to bring fruits. For this, the reason of "they were important" and "they were not part of that counsel" is sufficient, meaning they did not strive for the mission to be fulfilled specifically by them.

5. It can be said that Rashi’s view in his commentary on the Torah is not so. Rather, "Be courageous and take of the fruit of the land" was a personal obligation (as the simple language implies), and there was essentially an obligation on each of the twelve men to take and carry the fruit of the land [as we find also an opinion in Chazal that all of them carried fruits, including Joshua and Caleb].

Therefore, Rashi did not adopt the first reason in the Shas, that Joshua and Caleb did not carry anything "because they were important," because he holds that they were obligated by the personal duty of "take of the fruit of the land."

For this reason, Rashi cannot be satisfied with the language of the Shas that the reason they did not carry was "because they were not part of that counsel"—for this language can be interpreted (as mentioned above) as meaning they did not want there to be any room for error that they had any connection to the counsel of the spies in general—because this is not sufficient to override an explicit command of Moshe Rabbeinu, "take of the fruit of the land."

Therefore, Rashi wrote, "Joshua and Caleb did not carry anything because their entire intent was to bring an evil report; just as their fruit was unusual, so too were their people unusual." It can be said that Rashi does not mean that the spies explicitly said to the Children of Israel, "just as their fruit is unusual, etc." (for as mentioned, it is explicit at the beginning of their speech, "And they told him and said, 'We came to the land... and it is flowing with milk and honey, and this is its fruit,'" and Rashi explains that this was the "bit of truth" at the beginning that the spies said in order to sustain the lie they came to say afterward, "However, the people are strong, etc."). Rather, Rashi’s intent is that this was only their intent in bringing the fruits. The very act of bringing the fruits was done in a way that it appeared to the Children of Israel that they were thereby fulfilling Moshe’s mission, "take of the fruit of the land," but they had a hidden intent: that by saying a bit of truth, it would validate the lie of "However, the people are strong, etc." (because the Israelites would say, "Just as their fruit is unusual, so too are their people unusual").

According to this approach, it is explained how it is possible that Joshua and Caleb did not carry anything:

If there were no intent to bring an evil report (on the part of the spies) in the act of bringing the fruits, it is obvious that Joshua and Caleb would have participated with them to fulfill Moshe’s command and mission, "Be courageous and take of the fruit of the land" (which is a personal obligation, as mentioned).

But since there was a good act here (bringing fruits that show the quality and excellence of the land) together with an undesirable intent, they could not carry anything. For if they were to participate in bringing fruits from the goodness of the land, they would be assisting in a transgression (the evil intent of the spies in the act

## 52

of bringing). And assisting in a transgression overrides the fulfillment of Moshe’s mission. Perhaps it is not an overriding, but rather that this was Moshe’s intent: that they should not take fruits in a manner of assisting in a transgression; on the contrary, that would nullify the intent of the mission.

6. One can draw a lesson for the service of man from this Rashi:

It is known that the primary error of the spies was that they wanted to perform their service primarily in thought and speech (Torah study and the intent of the mitzvos) and did not want to descend into the world of action and fulfill practical mitzvos that are dependent on the land. Since this is so, a person might err in the other direction and think that since the action is the essence, the intent of the heart and the thought of the mind are not so relevant.

Regarding this, Rashi teaches us that their hidden intent "to bring an evil report" caused the fulfillment of Moshe’s command to be transformed into a matter of transgression and evil speech.

Great is the good measure, for from here we learn how far the merit of good intent and desirable thought reaches in the performance of mitzvos. As the saying goes, a mitzvah without intent is like a body without a soul. The soul’s action in the body is not in a peripheral or minor matter; rather, the soul changes the reality of the body from the opposite of life to life. So too with the intent of the mitzvah: the intent gives life to the mitzvah.

The essence of this is through the study of the inner dimension of the Torah, the teachings of Chassidus, in which there is a foretaste of the revelation of the reasons for the Torah that will be in the future, as is known.

It can be said that in this same way is the obligation to believe in Moshiach and wait for his coming, which must be with inwardness and truth, from the depths of the heart.

This will hasten and speed the true Redemption and the fulfillment of the promise, "And I will give you a heart of flesh," speedily in our days, truly.

(From a Sicha of Motzaei Shabbos Parshas Shelach, 5738)

## 53

A. "And if you err and do not perform all these mitzvos that Hashem spoke to Moshe", and Rashi explains (from the Sifri on the verse): "The verse speaks of avodah zarah. Or perhaps it is not so, but rather [it refers to] one of all the mitzvos? The verse states, 'all these mitzvos'—[meaning] one mitzvah that is like all the mitzvos. Just as one who transgresses all the mitzvos casts off the yoke, breaks the covenant, and reveals faces [in the Torah], so too with this mitzvah, he casts off the yoke, breaks the covenant, and reveals faces. And which is this? Avodah zarah."

And in the following verse, "all that Hashem commanded you through Moshe, from the day that Hashem commanded and onwards for your generations," Rashi explains: "'All that He commanded, etc.'—this tells us that whoever acknowledges avodah zarah is like one who denies the entire Torah and all that the prophets prophesied, as it is said, 'from the day that Hashem commanded and onwards.'"

This means that the verses here contain two points regarding the severity of avodah zarah (because of which it is like all the mitzvos and the entire Torah): (1) In the verse "And if you err... all these mitzvos," the intent is that in avodah zarah, one "casts off the yoke, breaks the covenant, and reveals faces," just as one who transgresses all the mitzvos. (2) From the verse "all that He commanded, etc.," we learn that whoever acknowledges avodah zarah is like one who denies the entire Torah.

Now, the source for Rashi is (as mentioned) the Sifri on the verse, but in the Gemara they say: "'And if you err and do not perform all these mitzvos'—which mitzvah is equivalent to all the mitzvos? You must say, this is avodah zarah." And Rashi explains there: "For whoever acknowledges it is like one who denies the entire Torah." Thus, in his commentary on the Shas, Rashi explains that the fact that the first verse equates avodah zarah to all the mitzvos ("And if you err... all these mitzvos") is because "whoever acknowledges it is like one who denies the entire Torah".

This requires explanation: Why is it different in his commentary on the Torah, where he notes (following the Sifri on the verse) that there are two distinct points in the verses here?

## 54

B. Furthermore: Earlier in Parshas Mishpatim, on the verse, "And in all that I have said to you, be careful, and the name of other gods you shall not mention," Rashi explains: "To teach you that avodah zarah is equivalent to all the mitzvos, and one who is careful regarding it is like one who observes them all." Based on this, it is difficult to understand why Rashi says here, "This tells us that whoever acknowledges avodah zarah is like one who denies the entire Torah," which implies that this matter is learned specifically from here.

[It is forced to say that the novelty here is only in the second detail in the continuation of Rashi here, that one who acknowledges avodah zarah denies (not only the entire Torah, but also) "all that the prophets prophesied, as it is said, 'from the day that Hashem commanded and onwards'"—for the plain language and style of Rashi imply that there is a novelty also in the first point he brings, that one who acknowledges avodah zarah is like one who denies the entire Torah.]

C. We must also examine: 1) The intent of Rashi in saying, "One mitzvah that is like all the mitzvos; just as one who transgresses all the mitzvos casts off the yoke, breaks the covenant, and reveals faces, so too this mitzvah, etc."—what are these three matters that Rashi cites regarding the severity of "one who transgresses all the mitzvos" (and their parallels in one who serves avodah zarah)?

2) The matter of "casting off the yoke" alone (for just as one who transgresses all the mitzvos has cast off from himself (completely) the yoke of the Ribono shel Olam, so too the one who serves avodah zarah has cast off the yoke) should be sufficient to explain the reason why avodah zarah is like all the mitzvos—why did Rashi need to add "breaks the covenant and reveals faces"?

[And even though it is stated this way in the Sifri, it has already been explained many times that Rashi does not copy into his commentary anything except what is relevant to understanding the plain meaning of the verse.]

3) The matter of "revealing faces" that Rashi cites without explanation requires study. The Re'em here brings from Rashi in Maseches Kerisos [on the words of the Shas there that according to Rabbi, "All sins in the Torah... Yom Kippur atones for (even without teshuvah), except for casting off the yoke, revealing faces in the Torah, and breaking the covenant of flesh"] that "revealing faces" means "interpreting the Aggados in a derogatory manner, such as Menashe, etc.". It has already been challenged: what does this have to do with our case? "Is it necessary that everyone who serves avodah zarah interprets Aggados in a derogatory manner?". They brought from the commentary of Rabbeinu Hillel on the Sifri who explained, "And revealing faces in the Torah, meaning one who is brazen regarding the Torah so as not to fulfill it" [and similarly we find in Rashi in Maseches Shevuos, "Revealing faces in the Torah, he comes to the words of Torah

## 55

with brazenness and by displaying his insolence"].

Seemingly, it is difficult to explain it this way in Rashi on the Torah, which he wrote (as has been explained many times) in a clear style (without needing to add further explanations to them). If his intent in "revealing faces" were the meaning of "coming... with brazenness," he should have explained it; if in his commentary on the Shas he found it necessary to explain it, all the more so in his commentary on the Torah.

D. It can be said that the explanation for all this is:
In his three aforementioned commentaries regarding the equation of avodah zarah to all the mitzvos, Rashi speaks of three different matters:
1) Since the mitzvah of the prohibition of avodah zarah is connected with the belief in Hashem (for "one who serves avodah zarah denies the Ribono shel Olam"), it is the foundation of all the mitzvos (for without belief in the Commander of the mitzvos, the concept of mitzvos is inconceivable).
2) In addition to this, the mitzvah of avodah zarah itself (as a specific mitzvah) is equivalent to all the mitzvos, similar to what we find regarding other mitzvos (such as tzitzis, Shabbos, etc.).

It can be said that this is the distinction between what Rashi wrote in Parshas Mishpatim, "To teach you that avodah zarah is equivalent to all the mitzvos, and one who is careful regarding it is like one who observes them all," and what he wrote in our Parsha, "that whoever acknowledges avodah zarah is like one who denies the entire Torah":
In Parshas Mishpatim it says, "And in all that I have said to you, be careful, and the name of other gods you shall not mention." The fact that the verse warns of both matters (observing all the mitzvos and being careful regarding avodah zarah) in immediate succession indicates the equation between "all the mitzvos" and the mitzvah of avodah zarah. This is what Rashi emphasizes: "To teach you that avodah zarah is equivalent to all the mitzvos," meaning he refers to the second matter mentioned above, that the severity of avodah zarah is equivalent "to (the severity of) all the mitzvos."

However, in our Parsha, which discusses only the mitzvah of avodah zarah, and the verse details "all that Hashem commanded you through Moshe, from the day that Hashem commanded and onwards for your generations," we learn from this that a lack of care regarding avodah zarah harms the care for "all that Hashem commanded you, etc." This is what Rashi says: "This tells us that whoever acknowledges avodah zarah is like one who denies the entire Torah"—and his intent is the first matter mentioned above, that since he has denied the Giver of the Torah and the Commander of the mitzvos, he is automatically "like one who denies the entire Torah." This is not merely a description of the severity of the mitzvah or its equation to other mitzvos, but a description of reality—that one who acknowledges avodah zarah is "like one who (in practice) denies the entire Torah."

[Based on this, another change is understood—that in Parshas Mishpatim Rashi’s language is "equivalent to all the mitzvos," and in our Parsha he says "the entire Torah"—because there the emphasis is on the equation between one mitzvah and all the mitzvos, whereas in our Parsha the emphasis is on the denial of the content of all the mitzvos, and therefore he says "like one who denies the entire Torah," which does not refer only to the performance of the mitzvos, but to all matters of Torah given to Moshe (and as the continuation of Rashi’s words, that this also includes "all that the prophets prophesied").]

E. But these two matters are not sufficient to explain the language of the verse, "And if you err and do not perform all these mitzvos," for the plain meaning of the language is that it speaks of a transgression of "all these mitzvos." Because from the perspective that the severity of avodah zarah is "equivalent" to the severity of all the mitzvos, one cannot say "and do not perform all these mitzvos"; and even from the perspective that "whoever acknowledges avodah zarah is like one who denies the entire Torah," it is seemingly not appropriate to say that one who serves avodah zarah did not perform "all these mitzvos" [because this is only a matter of belief in the heart, that one who acknowledges avodah zarah is "like one who denies the entire Torah," but not that one who acknowledges avodah zarah is considered as having not performed "all these mitzvos"].

Therefore, Rashi says that there is an additional matter in avodah zarah: that in the prohibition of avodah zarah there is the same content that exists in the nullification of all the mitzvos, for "just as one who transgresses all the mitzvos casts off the yoke, breaks the covenant, and reveals faces, so too this mitzvah, he casts off the yoke, breaks the covenant, and reveals faces."

Explanation of the matter:
There are three general categories in the fulfillment of mitzvos: 1) Acceptance of His yoke, 2) Fulfillment of the sign of the covenant that the Ribono shel Olam made with Bnei Yisrael, 3) Conduct in a straight path. It can be said that these are the three types of mitzvos—chukim, eidus, and mishpatim: The goal of chukim, "matters that are the King's decree," is that a person should accept upon himself the yoke of the Ribono shel Olam; mitzvos that are in the category of "eidus"—which are "testimony" and a remembrance of the kindnesses of the Makom or other matters in the relationship between the Ribono shel Olam and His people Yisrael—are in the category of keeping a "covenant" (like the covenant which is a sign and remembrance between those who make the covenant); and mishpatim are "matters stated in the Torah as judgments, which if they had not been stated, would have been worthy of being stated," straight conduct, etc.

It can be said that these are the three matters in Rashi’s commentary here, that one who transgresses all the mitzvos (1) casts off the yoke—corresponding to chukim; (2) breaks the covenant—corresponding to eidus; (3) reveals faces—"faces" in the sense of the expression "we will explain the faces [aspects] of the halacha," "he shows him faces," and the like, which means providing a reason and explanation, or—"faces" from the word "ofanim" (wheels/modes) (as Rashi says in many places in his commentary on the Torah: "translated with other faces [aspects]," "it has many faces," and the like); and "revealing faces" means that he invents new "faces" and reasons (and modes), through which he "bends" and distorts the intellect of the Torah—and this is corresponding to mishpatim, which are intellectual mitzvos.

Based on this, it can be said that the fact that Rashi wrote that one who serves avodah zarah "casts off the yoke, breaks the covenant, and reveals faces" is not only because avodah zarah is connected with denial of Hashem, and therefore it automatically contains the casting off of the yoke, breaking of the covenant, and revealing of faces, but rather that in the mitzvah of avodah zarah itself, there are these three traits of casting off the yoke, breaking the covenant, and revealing faces.

That is to say: The mitzvah of avodah zarah is obligated from all three perspectives of "yoke," "covenant," and "faces" (chukim, eidus, and mishpatim)—because: Belief in Hashem is necessary from the perspective of intellect, and one who serves avodah zarah (and denies Hashem) is "revealing faces," the opposite of straight intellect; and in addition to this, the verse emphasizes "I am Hashem your G-d who brought you out of the land of Egypt"—that the acceptance of the Kingship of the Ribono shel Olam is a sign and remembrance of the fact that the Ribono shel Olam brought us out of Egypt (like the matter of "eidus" and the covenant); and furthermore—"who brought you out of the land of Egypt from the house of bondage," "the exodus is worthy that you should be enslaved to Me," that the acceptance of His Kingship must be in the manner of accepting His yoke, like a servant who accepts upon himself the yoke of his master, regardless of whether he understands the reason or not, etc.

***

F. Now, regarding avodah zarah, there are several versions—sometimes the term "avodas kochavim" (or "akum") is used, sometimes "avodas elilim" ("avodah elilim"), and sometimes "avodah zarah" ("avodah zarah"). Even though it is difficult to clarify what is the correct version, and what was changed by the censor, etc., it is logical to say that the correct version is "avodah zarah," as the name of the Masechta in the Shas [and note the words of Chazal, "He thought it meant literal avodah zarah, but it is not so, rather it is avodah zarah—service that is strange to him"].

We must examine the content of this name "avodah zarah," for seemingly this name does not emphasize the severity of the transgression like the name "avodas elilim" or "avodas kochavim umazalos," just as a Yisrael is called "zar" (stranger) relative to a Kohen.

This can be understood based on the words of the Rambam at the beginning of Hilchos Avodah Zarah, where he explains how the matter of avodah zarah unfolded in the world, starting from the great mistake that people made in the days of Enosh, saying that since the Ribono shel Olam gave honor to the stars and they are ministers serving before Him, "they are worthy to be praised and glorified and to have honor given to them, and this is the will of the G-d, blessed be He, to magnify and honor those whom He has magnified and honored, just as a king wishes to honor those who stand before him, and this is the honor of the king."

Seemingly, one must understand—what is the great deficiency and flaw in honoring the ministers of the Ribono shel Olam through whom the Ribono shel Olam conducts the world? As we see in the kingdom of the earth, that even though all the power and strength of the king's ministers is only from the king himself (and it is in his power to replace them, etc.), nevertheless, for most matters of the conduct of the state, one must ask the ministers specifically and not go to the king himself (unless it is a matter of the greatest importance).

The simple reason for this is because they have no free choice at all whether to influence or not, and they are only like an axe in the hand of the one who hews with it, for one does not thank the axe but only the one who hews with it [unlike in the kingdom of the earth, where the king's ministers, even though all their strength and power are from the king, are possessors of free choice and it is in their hands to change, etc.].

But it is known in the explanation of the prayer "the attribute of mercy, roll upon us," and the like—that this is similar to an advocate and the like, even though "you are children to Hashem your G-d," "My firstborn son Yisrael," and it is said, "For what great nation is there that has G-d close to it as Hashem our G-d is whenever we call to Him," and Chazal said, "We call to Him and not to His attributes" (and all the more so not to those who serve before Him).

This is, in the most subtle of subtleties, the explanation of the term "avodah zarah"—that when a Yisrael calls only to those who serve before Him, this is a matter of "zarus" (strangeness), like a "zar" who has no closeness to the King Himself, unlike Bnei Yisrael who are a people close to Him, who ask and call to the Ribono shel Olam Himself (and the attribute of mercy—as an advocate), and certainly the Ribono shel Olam fulfills their request, "like a child who pleads before his father and he does his will."

(From the sicha of Shabbos Parshas Shelach, 5729)


## קרח

## Sicha 1

### 60

A. Regarding the laws of *bitul* of *terumah* in a ratio of one hundred and one (where the *terumah* "is nullified in one hundred and one"), we learned in Maseches Terumos: Rabbi Yehoshua says black figs nullify white ones, and white ones nullify black ones (if a black *terumah* fig or a white one fell into one hundred *chullin* figs, half of which are black and half white, they all combine to nullify it. If a black one fell, one of the black ones nullifies it; if a white one fell, one of the white ones nullifies it, and the rest remain *chullin* as they were. R' Ovadiah of Bartenura). Rabbi Eliezer forbids it (if a black one fell, the black ones are forbidden; if a white one fell, the white ones are forbidden. R' Ovadiah of Bartenura), and Rabbi Akiva says: if it is known what fell (black or white), they do not nullify each other, but if it is not known what fell, they do nullify each other (and in the Rambam, the *halacha* is decided according to Rabbi Akiva, that "if it is not known whether it was black or white, it is nullified in one hundred and one of the whole").

The reasoning of Rabbi Eliezer, who forbids it in this case, is seemingly simple: in reality, the *terumah* was only mixed into fifty (if white — among the fifty white ones; if black — among the fifty black ones), and in either case, there are not one hundred of the species that fell.

The commentators write that Rabbi Yehoshua holds that since "if he wanted, he could crush them and mix them all together," therefore they all combine to nullify it [and even though one may not nullify an *issur* *l'chatchilah*, the Tosafos Yom Tov explains that "this applies to increasing the *heter* in order to nullify the *issur*, but in a case like this, where he is not increasing the *heter* but rather changing the *issur* from how it was and separating them as they were before in order to mix them together, in such a case it seems to me that it is permitted to nullify *l'chatchilah*"].

Regarding the reasoning of Rabbi Akiva, who distinguishes between when it is known what fell and when it is not known, they write that when it is known which fig fell, black or white, "they do not nullify, because he can eat the others, and since they are permitted, they do not assist in nullifying. But if it is not known whether a black one fell or a white one fell, since they are all in a state of *safek issur*, they nullify each other."

## 61

One must understand the reasoning of Rabbi Yehoshua — what difference does it make that "if he wanted, he could crush them and mix them all together"? In practice, he did not grind or crush them; how then do they nullify each other?

Similarly, it requires study regarding the opinion of Rabbi Akiva — what difference does it make that the person does not know whether it was black or white and that "they are all in a state of *safek issur*"? This *safek* exists only in the person's knowledge, but in reality, the *terumah* was not mixed into one hundred (for a white fig does not mix with a black fig (and vice versa), and in the words of the Tosafos, "the black ones are not fit to be in a state of *safek dimu'a*, for the black ones are always distinguishable from the white ones"); there is no mixture of one and one hundred here?

B. It can be said that Rabbi Eliezer, Rabbi Yehoshua, and Rabbi Akiva disagree on the general definition of *bitul* in a mixture of dry with dry — which is not like a mixture of wet with wet. The latter is a literal mixture, where they become one entity that cannot be separated. This is not the case with dry with dry, where each remains separate, but the person does not know where the *issur* is located. The definition of this mixture can be explained in two ways:

1) The definition of a dry-with-dry mixture is indeed different from a wet-with-wet mixture; it is not a mixture in the *cheftza* (the object itself), but only from the perspective of the *gavra* (the person). In the person's knowledge, there is a mixture of *issur* and *heter*, and this is sufficient to nullify the *issur*.

2) Even a dry-with-dry mixture is in the *cheftza*, because the definition of "mixture" is that it is impossible to distinguish between the *heter* and the *issur*.

This is the reason for the dispute between Rabbi Eliezer (who forbids) and Rabbi Yehoshua and Rabbi Akiva:

Rabbi Eliezer holds that even the definition of a dry-with-dry mixture must be in the *cheftza*. Therefore, he holds that the white ones cannot nullify the black ones (and vice versa), as there is no mixture in reality, as mentioned above in the words of the Tosafos, that they cannot be mixed because they are always distinguishable on their own.

In contrast, Rabbi Yehoshua and Rabbi Akiva hold that a dry-with-dry mixture is sufficient even when it is only from the perspective of the *gavra*, in the knowledge of the person (who does not know where the *issur* is). This is also the reasoning of Rabbi Akiva, who holds that "when it is not known what fell, they nullify each other" — due to the mixture in the person's knowledge; in the case at hand, in his knowledge, they are mixed throughout the entire hundred.

Based on this, one can also explain the reasoning of Rabbi Yehoshua, who holds that they all combine to nullify it because "if he wanted, he could crush them and mix them all together" (even though he did not crush and mix them): The crushing of figs is (not merely a theoretical "if he wanted," but) the standard way to crush figs; and since a dry-with-dry mixture is...

## 63

...in another style: In order for the person's knowledge to effect a law in the *cheftza*, the black and white figs, which are not mixed by their own nature, must undergo a change in the figs themselves—that a black or white one fell—which creates a mixture in reality, where the black fig is among the black ones. Then, when it is not known which of them fell, the mixture in the person's mind effects a mixture in the figs into which the *terumah* fig fell. But when he knew and forgot, the person's forgetfulness does not mix the figs that are not mixed in reality.

D. One can explain the reason why this matter—that the knowledge and thought of a person has the power to act upon an object outside of himself—is explicitly stated specifically regarding *terumah*. The foundation of the law that thought is effective is explicit regarding *terumah*, as we learn from the verse, "And your *terumah* shall be reckoned unto you, as the corn of the threshing floor," meaning "by thought alone it shall be *terumah*."

And even though the case at hand is not about the law of separating and sanctifying *terumah*, but about the law of the nullification of *terumah* (where the figs are permitted for eating), it nevertheless stems from the fact that in the laws of *terumah*, human thought has a special power.

More deeply, it can be said (by way of *remez*) that even through the permitting of all the figs for a Jew to eat, an elevation is effected in them similar to the separation of *terumah*. This is precise in the language of the Mishnah, "they nullify each other," even though the intent is that they are permitted for eating; for the very fact that the figs are permitted for eating for a Jew effects an elevation in them, even before the person eats them.

This is similar to the concept of *terumah* that exists in thought, for even according to the opinions that thought does not effect that it should be *terumah*, it is at least effective to permit the *tevel* for eating, by him setting his eyes on this side and eating from that side, and this too is learned from the verse "and it shall be reckoned."

A similar thing applies in the case at hand: the knowledge and thought of the person acts upon an object outside of himself so that it becomes permitted for eating. And from the perspective of its being made fit for eating by a Jew, this itself is an elevation of the object, as mentioned above.

From this, there is a lesson regarding the great value of a person's knowledge and thought—from which one should learn both on the negative side, how careful one must be of improper thought, for in addition to the gravity of the matter itself, it also harms the world around him, and—even more so, for the attribute of good is greater—the great elevation and necessity of good thought, to think of the good of others, for in addition to helping through actual deed or speech, thought also helps a great deal.

## Sicha 2

1. At the conclusion of the laws of *terumas maaser* (at the end of our *parsha*), it is stated: "And you shall bear no sin by reason of it, when you have heaved from it the best of it; and you shall not profane the holy things of the Children of Israel, lest you die." Rashi comments: "And you shall bear no sin — this implies that if you do not heave, you will bear sin. Lest you die — this implies that if you profane, you will die." The simple intent of Rashi (as explained by the commentators) is that the verse is not coming to inform us that if the *Leviim* heave the *terumas maaser* they will not sin or be punished—for what would that teach us?—but rather it is teaching us, through negative phrasing, the prohibition and the punishment: "If you do not heave, you will bear sin," and "if you profane, you will die."

Now, the lengthiness of the verse and Rashi’s comment implies that there are two distinct matters (prohibitions) here: (a) "And you shall bear no sin by reason of it, when you have heaved..." and (b) "And you shall not profane the holy things of the Children of Israel..." For if one were to say that the entire verse discusses a single matter, and that "bearing sin" is the very same punishment of death mentioned at the end of the verse, it should have said, "And you shall not bear sin by reason of it, to die" (or similar)—similar to the phrasing of the verse earlier in our *parsha*, "And they shall not approach... the Tent of Meeting, to bear sin, and die," and similarly earlier in *Parshas Emor* (regarding the prohibition of eating *terumah* in a state of *tumah*), it is stated, "And they shall not bear sin by reason of it, and die thereby."

It requires study: why does Rashi remain silent and not explain what the two prohibitions in this verse are? Especially since we find several explanations in *Chazal* and the biblical commentators regarding the duplication in the verse, whereas in Rashi, seemingly, there is no mention of a resolution to this question.

2. On the face of it, one could say that Rashi does not need to explain it, because it is understood from the language of the verse itself: "And you shall not bear sin by reason of it, when you have heaved from it the best of it" refers to *maaser* from which *terumas maaser* has not been heaved, for "if you do not heave, you will bear sin"; and the end of the verse, "And you shall not profane the holy things of the Children of Israel," refers to a *zar* (an Israelite or a *Levi*) who eats *terumas maaser*, as the verse states, "And you shall not profane the holy things of the Children of Israel" (which refers to something that is already holy), and the profanation of the holy is through the eating of the holy.

However, it is a great strain to say that according to Rashi, the verse "And you shall not profane the holy things of the Children of Israel, lest you die" refers to a *zar* who eats *terumas maaser*, because according to Rashi, this matter has already been learned from what was written earlier: "And your *terumah* shall be reckoned to you as the corn from the threshing floor," and as Rashi comments there: "Your *terumas maaser* is forbidden to *zarim* and to the *tamei*, and they are liable for it to death and a *chomesh*, like *terumah gedolah*" (and it is included in the warning of *terumah gedolah*).

Many of the literal commentators explain that the statement, "And you shall not profane the holy things of the Children of Israel," is the prohibition (and punishment) for eating *maaser* from which *terumas maaser* has not been taken. And seemingly, since Rashi is silent and does not specify to which matter "And you shall not profane the holy things of the Children of Israel" is said—and this *parsha* discusses only the laws of *terumas maaser*—it must be said that Rashi also holds this view. For if it does not refer to the prohibition of eating *maaser* from which *terumas maaser* has not been taken (which is learned from "And your *terumah* shall be reckoned to you," as mentioned above), it must be that it speaks of the prohibition of eating *maaser* from which *terumas maaser* has not been taken.

But if so (in addition to the fact that the simple meaning of the verse "And the holy things of the Children of Israel" implies that it refers to something already holy), it still requires explanation: what is the reason for the duplication in this verse?

3. Seemingly, the duplication can be explained by saying that "And you shall not bear sin by reason of it, when you have heaved from it the best of it" refers to the essence of the prohibition of not heaving *terumas maaser* (even if one does not eat the *maaser* that is *tovel* to *terumas maaser*), as indicated by the phrasing, "If you do not heave, you will bear sin." Whereas "And you shall not profane the holy things of the Children of Israel" is the prohibition of eating *maaser* that is *tovel* to *terumas maaser*.

But it is difficult to explain it this way, because if so, what does "And you shall not bear sin by reason of it" teach us? The very prohibition of not heaving *terumas maaser* is known from the obligation and the *mitzvah* of "And you shall heave from it the *terumah* of Hashem, a *maaser* from the *maaser*," and it is understood that if one does not heave, one transgresses a *mitzvah* of commission.

Furthermore: simply put, it should be said that "And you shall not bear sin by reason of it" means that there is a punishment for it, because the expression "bearing sin" generally and in most cases indicates a matter of punishment. But if you say that it refers to not heaving *terumas maaser*, it is extremely difficult, for is it possible to say that there is a punishment of death (by Heaven) for the neglect of separating *terumas maaser*? And it is likewise difficult to say that "you shall bear sin" means the punishment of *malkos*, for there is no warning here, and furthermore—there is no action in the failure to heave *terumas maaser* (and one is not lashed for a negative commandment that lacks an action).

Some commentators explain that "And you shall not bear sin by reason of it" refers to the prohibition of eating *maaser* that is *tovel* to *terumas maaser* [and according to this, the statement "And you shall not bear sin by reason of it, when you have heaved..."—"for if you do not heave, you will bear sin"—means that "if you do not heave," you will come to sin when you eat the *maaser*]. And according to this, it works out well that there is a "bearing of sin" here, a punishment for the act of eating.

But if this is Rashi’s view in his commentary on the Torah, the difficulty returns to its place—since both parts of the verse discuss one prohibition (eating *maaser* that is *tovel* to *terumas maaser*)—what is the duplication in the verse? And furthermore: what "bearing of sin"—punishment—is there here, other than the punishment of death ("lest you die") that is at the end of the verse?

And one cannot say that "And you shall not bear sin by reason of it" means the punishment of *malkos*—for since there is a punishment of death for eating *tevel* to *terumas maaser* ("lest you die"), there is no law of *malkos* here, because "he is exempt from the lesser punishment" (which is a simple logical principle even according to the literal approach).

[And even though the punishment of death here is only death by Heaven, and it is only according to Abaye (in the opinion of R' Nechunya ben HaKaneh) that we say *kal v'chomer* in this case—nevertheless, according to the literal meaning of the verse, there is no room to distinguish between death by man and death by Heaven, for the logic of "he is exempt from the lesser punishment"...]

6. Based on all the above, the duplication in the verse "And you shall not bear sin by reason of it, when you have heaved... and you shall not profane the holy things of the Children of Israel..." is resolved—even though both refer to *maaser* that is *tovel* to *terumas maaser*:

According to what we explained above, that the definition of the prohibition is from the side of the *terumas maaser* portion within it, it follows that there are two prohibitions in eating *maaser* that is *tovel* to *terumas maaser*: (a) The prohibition of *gezeilah* (theft)—that one is stealing from the *Kohanim* the portion of *terumas maaser* within it, for since the holiness of *terumas maaser* has already taken effect upon it, it can be said that this portion already belongs to the *Kohanim* (even before it is given to the *Kohen*). (b) The prohibition of profaning holy things, for the holiness of *terumas maaser* has taken effect upon it (and it is called "the *terumah* of Hashem").

And these two prohibitions are what are specified in the verse here: (a) "And you shall not bear sin by reason of it, when you have heaved from it the best of it"—"for if you do not heave, you will bear sin," which is the prohibition of stealing from the *Kohanim*, and therefore it says "when you have heaved" ("for if you do not heave"), because the theft occurs immediately when the *Levi* does not heave and separate the portion of *terumas maaser* within the *maaser* [except that as long as he has not eaten it (and the like), it is in his power to separate it and return the stolen property, as below in section 7]; (b) "And you shall not profane the holy things of the Children of Israel," which is the prohibition of eating the *maaser* that is *tovel* because of the holiness of the *terumah* within it ("the holy things of the Children of Israel").

And this is also the distinction: regarding "And you shall not profane," it says "lest you die," because the punishment for profaning holy things is death (by Heaven), whereas regarding "when you have heaved from it the best of it," it says simply "and you shall not bear sin by reason of it," because there is nothing here but the prohibition of *gezeilah*.

7. It can be said that the statement "And you shall not bear sin by reason of it," which indicates a matter of punishment (and not merely a prohibition of *gezeilah*), refers to the obligation of payment to the *Kohen* (for the obligation of payment is also included in "bearing sin"). For since the holiness of *terumas maaser* has already taken effect upon it and it belongs to the *Kohanim*, therefore, one who destroys *maaser* that is *tovel* to *terumas maaser* is obligated to pay the *Kohanim* the tenth part (*maaser* from the *maaser*) that is within it.

And even though as long as the *maaser* exists, he can separate *terumas maaser* from it and return the stolen property—if he destroyed the *maaser* (by eating or in any other way) without heaving *terumas maaser* from it, then he is obligated to pay the *Kohen*.

[According to the law, there is a specific limitation that the *Kohen* has nothing until the time of heaving and onwards (even according to the one who says that gifts that were not heaved are considered as if they were heaved). But according to the literal approach, it can be said that this is not so, for since the *terumas maaser* is mixed into the *maaser* and the holiness of *terumas maaser* has taken effect upon it, this portion already belongs to the *Kohanim* (even before it is given to the *Kohen* in practice) and one is obligated to pay the *Kohen* for its destruction.]

And even though before the giving to the *Kohen* it is impossible to say that it is in the possession of a specific *Kohen*, nevertheless, it can be said that according to the literal approach, it has already become the property of the *Kohanim* (and it is only that the *Levi* has the benefit of choice to give it to whichever *Kohen* he wishes, and this is the *mitzvah* of giving *terumas maaser*), and he is obligated to pay. And it can be said that he gives it to whichever *Kohen* he wishes.

On the other hand, it can be said: since when the *terumas maaser* has not yet reached the hand of a *Kohen*, an obligation of payment does not apply to it (because it is not in the ownership of a specific *Kohen*)—for this very reason, it can be said that according to the literal approach, one who destroys *maaser* that is *tovel* is liable for *malkos* for the negative commandment of *gezeilah*. For the reason that one is not lashed for the negative commandment of *gezeilah* is because it is a negative commandment that is rectified by a positive commandment ("and he shall return the stolen property"), for which one is not lashed (as is mentioned (also) in Rashi on the Torah), but if he has already eaten the stolen property, it is no longer rectified by a positive commandment, and since in our case there is also no payment (as mentioned above), then he is lashed for the theft of the *Kohanim*.

And according to this, it turns out that "And you shall not bear sin..." means exactly as it sounds (as in most places), that there is an actual punishment here.

And perhaps it can be said even further, that even if he destroys the *maaser* by eating, in which case he is liable to the punishment of death by Heaven ("lest you die"), he is nevertheless obligated in payment (or *malkos*), and we do not say *kal v'chomer* in this case—for since the payment (or *malkos*) is from the side of the theft of the *Kohen*, while the punishment of death is from the side of the prohibition of profaning holy things, then this is death for one and payment for the other, and according to the literal approach, we do not say *kal v'chomer* in this case.

(From the Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Korach, 5725)


## חוקת

## Sicha 1

A. The Rambam writes: "From where do we know that a *tzamid patil* (a tightly sealed lid) protects against the *tumah* of a corpse in a tent? As it is stated: 'And every open vessel which has no *tzamid patil* upon it is *tamei*,' implying that if it does have a *tzamid patil* upon it, it is *tahor*. And by tradition, it was learned that the verse speaks only of an earthenware vessel (*kli cheres*), a vessel that only contracts *tumah* through its opening. Therefore, if its opening was sealed and covered with a *tzamid patil*, it protects everything inside it. A *kal v’chomer* applies to all vessels that do not contract *tumah* that they should protect with a *tzamid patil* (and these are: vessels of dung, stone, earth, fish bones, fish skin, bird bones, and wooden vessels that come with a measurement, wooden boards which are flat and not considered vessels, and unfinished metal vessels)."

Further on (two chapters later), he writes: "All vessels that protect with a *tzamid patil* protect everything within them, whether food, liquids, garments, or *kli shetef* (vessels of fabric/leather/wood). This is the law of the Torah. However, by rabbinic decree, an earthenware vessel covered with a *tzamid patil* protects only food, liquids, and other earthenware vessels within it. But if *kli shetef* or garments were inside the earthenware vessel, they are *tamei*. And why did they say it should not protect everything like other vessels that protect? Because other vessels that protect do not contract *tumah*, whereas earthenware vessels do contract *tumah*, and a *tamei* vessel cannot serve as a partition (*chatzitzah*). And since every vessel of an *am ha'aretz* is presumed to be *tamei*, they decreed that an earthenware vessel should not protect *kli shetef* inside it."

The Rambam continues: "A person who was inside a barrel covered with a *tzamid patil* is *tahor*... and it appears to me that they did not decree that an earthenware vessel should not protect a person, because it is an uncommon occurrence, and they did not issue decrees regarding uncommon occurrences."

Further on, he continues: "A chimney (a window) between a house and an attic, with *tumah* in the house and an earthenware pot placed over the opening of the chimney... everything in the attic, including food, liquids, and earthenware vessels, is *tahor*. But a person and *kli shetef* in the attic are *tamei*, for an earthenware vessel only serves as a partition for food, liquids, and earthenware vessels. Anything else in the attic is as if it were under a *tzamid patil* in an earthenware vessel, and it makes the person in that attic *tamei*, because this is a common occurrence."

## 73

Thus, we find that in this law of protection from the *tumah* of a corpse in a tent via *tzamid patil*, there are three categories:
1) Vessels that do not contract *tumah* protect everything inside them with a *tzamid patil*, even according to rabbinic law.
2) An earthenware vessel covered with a *tzamid patil* protects food and liquids, and also protects a person inside the barrel, but by rabbinic law, it does not protect *kli shetef* and garments.
3) A person in an attic with a pot over the chimney—in this case, the earthenware vessel does not protect the person in the attic.

B. It is known that from every law in the Torah, one must learn instructions for *avodas Hashem*. That is, in addition to the simple meaning of every law (which is the primary one), there is also a meaning in spiritual *avodah* that applies to everyone. In the words of the Rambam: "Most of the laws of the Torah are nothing but advice from the Great Counselor to refine one's character and straighten all one's deeds." As it is written: "And Hashem commanded us to perform all these statutes to fear Hashem," meaning the purpose of "all these statutes" is that through them one will come to "fear Hashem." Therefore, one must learn from every law an instruction regarding the fear of Heaven.

Likewise in our case, these three matters regarding protection from the *tumah* of a corpse in a tent have parallels in a person's *avodah* to their Creator.

C. Explanation of the matter:
The concept of "life" in spirituality and in a person's *avodah* means *dveikus* in Hashem, as it is written: "And you who cleave to Hashem your G-d are all alive today." The Holy One, Blessed be He, is called "Life," and furthermore, He is the source of life, the "Life-giver of life." Therefore, the Jewish people are called "alive," because they are attached to the Holy One, Blessed be He, for their soul is "a literal part of G-d from above," and as the saying goes, "embraced and attached to You."

From this, it follows that the absence of *dveikus* in *Elokus*, G-d forbid, is a state of "death," the opposite of life. Accordingly, one can say that the concept of the "tent of the dead" in spirituality refers to the entirety of this world (before its refinement), which is called the "world of *kelipos* and *sitra achra*" (for this world "is full of *kelipos* and *sitra achra* which are literally against Hashem"). And a *kelipah* "is a place of death and *tumah*, may Hashem protect us."

Thus, the descent of the human soul into this world is analogous to a person being in the tent of the dead.

Now, although the soul itself, being a pure soul attached to Hashem, is "life" that the "corpse-tumah" of this world cannot affect, nevertheless, through the soul's enclothement in a physical body—which creates a connection between the soul and the world outside it—

## 74

it is possible for the *tumah* of this world to blemish its holiness, and it requires a form of guarding ("protection") from this *tumah*.

In general, the soul's *dveikus* with the Holy One, Blessed be He, while it is below in this world, is through the engagement in Torah and *mitzvos*: "See, I have set before you today life and good... therefore choose life." However, this is a guarding and protection during the time one is engaged in Torah and *mitzvos*. This is not the case during times when one is engaged in the needs of the body and one's livelihood, etc.; at those times, one requires additional protection from the *tumah* of this world. This guarding and protection occurs when one changes the essence of the body, until it reaches a state where it is completely guarded from the *tumah* of this world.

This is the hint in the details of the laws regarding the different ways that vessels protect from *tumah* in the tent of the dead. They are "advice... from the Great Counselor" on how a person can protect themselves from the *tumah* of this world ("the tent of the dead"). There are different ways to do this:

The "vessel" is the human body, in which the soul is enclothed. An ordinary vessel contracts *tumah* in the tent of the dead; that is, the body by itself is affected by its environment, and consequently, the *tumah* spreads to the person's soul and damages its holiness. But there are ways that the "vessel"—the body—can protect from the *tumah* of the tent. In general, there are two ways:

The first way is when the vessel does not contract *tumah*, a vessel that "is not considered a vessel regarding *tumah*." Its spiritual meaning is when the primary *avodah* of the person is the *avodah* of the soul, where the light of the soul shines within them in a revealed manner. The body not only does not conceal the light of the soul, but it does not even limit it (to such an extent). Therefore, it does not have the status of a "vessel" (regarding the laws of *tumah*), since this body does not limit the light of the soul.

The second way is when the vessel is an earthenware vessel, made of earth. Its meaning in *avodah* is the matter of *bitul* ("And I am but dust and ashes," "And let my soul be as dust to all"), where the body has no existence (of its own), but is completely *batel* to the soul, in such a way that the holiness of the soul permeates all the details of the body. Consequently, there is no place for any matter of *tumah*, which is the opposite of the life and holiness of the soul.

That is to say: In the first way, from the outset, there is no limitation from the side of the body, and the entire *avodah* is the *avodah* of the soul ("those who serve Hashem with their souls"). Therefore, the *tumah* of this world does not affect them, for since they are in a "vessel" that is not in the category of *tumah* (as their body is not a "vessel" that limits the revelation of their soul's light), it is as if they are not in the "tent of the dead" (the place of *tumah*).

This is not the case in the second way, where the body is in the category of a "vessel" that limits the revelation of the soul's light [which is why an earthenware vessel is a vessel that can contract *tumah* (at least from its airspace), because the vessel causes a limitation in the soul's light]. However, when a person performs their *avodah* with the body (the vessel) itself, to refine and purify it until it is perfectly refined, so that the body has no existence of its own, and the entire purpose of the body is solely to reveal the light of its soul, then they are saved from the *tumah* that is in the tent of the dead.

In both of these ways, the Torah says that the vessel (the body) can only protect from the *tumah* of the tent of the dead when there is a "tzamid patil upon it"—that is, when it is entirely immersed in this *avodah* [whether by elevating itself above the measurements and limitations of its body, until its body is not in the category of a "vessel"; or the *avodah* of refining and purifying the body itself, until it is in the category of "earthenware," "dust and ashes," completely *batel* to the soul]. If, however, there is an "opening" to the air of the world, then it is not saved from the *tumah* of the tent of the dead.

In this way, we can explain the inner reason for another distinction we find between all the vessels that protect and earthenware vessels: "All vessels that protect with a *tzamid patil*, if they were a *tefach* by a *tefach* by a *tefach* high and were turned over on their openings on the ground, even if they were not sealed with a *tzamid patil* on the sides, they protect everything beneath them down to the abyss, because they are like a tent, and a tent protects. This is not the case if the vessel was an earthenware vessel, for the tents of earthenware vessels do not protect."

In other vessels that are not in the category of *tumah* (and in spiritual *avodah*—this means a body that does not conceal the light of the soul and does not give place to the *tumah* of this world, etc.), it is appropriate to say that when they are turned over on their openings and made into a tent of their own, it is again considered that they are not in the tent of the dead (for this is enough to cancel the "opening" to the air of the world, and there is no connection between the body and the *tumah* of this world surrounding it).

This is not the case with an earthenware vessel, for since the matter of *tumah* is not negated from them (and in spiritual *avodah*—this means a body that is not negated from the *tumah* of this world)—and the fact that a "tzamid patil" protects from *tumah* is only because it actually partitions against the *tumah* and does not allow the *tumah* to spread inside the vessel—therefore, simply turning the vessel over on its opening is not enough to partition against the *tumah* of the tent of the dead. There must be a strong barrier, a "tzamid patil," for only then is it considered that there is no "opening" to the air of the world surrounding it.

D. This is also the hint in the distinction between vessels that do not contract *tumah* and earthenware vessels, where the Sages decreed that earthenware vessels should not protect *kli shetef* or garments inside them, unlike vessels that do not contract *tumah*, where they did not issue this decree:

In the first way, where there is no connection to the *avodah* of the body itself, and it is as if they are not in the tent of the dead as mentioned above, then they are not in the category of *tumah*, and therefore there is no need for a decree. This is not the case in the second way, which is *avodah* within the body; after all, there is a possibility for the matter of *tumah* (since their *avodah* is with the body itself). Therefore, the Sages, who added fences and safeguards, decreed that earthenware vessels should not protect against *tumah*.

However, this decree is only regarding *kli shetef* and garments, which are things that are not part of the person themselves but are things outside of them. Therefore, even though they are connected to them (as hinted by the fact that they are inside the earthenware vessel), nevertheless, the holiness of their soul does not shine in them as much, and therefore the Sages decreed that they are not saved from the *tumah* of the tent.

This is not the case for a person who is inside an earthenware vessel—for a person (from the word *adamah l'elyon*, "I will be like the Most High") refers to the soul of the person—they are not included in this decree, for the soul itself, being inside an earthenware vessel that has a "tzamid patil upon it" (meaning the body is perfectly *batel* to it), there is no concern that the *tumah* of this world will touch it, G-d forbid. Likewise, they did not decree regarding food and liquids inside an earthenware vessel, because they become blood and flesh like one's own flesh and become unified with one's body. And since one's body is at the level of an "earthenware vessel," therefore the food and liquids that become part of one's body are also saved from the *tumah* of this world.

It can be said that this explanation also fits with the reason according to the revealed Torah for this decree—because of the concern of the vessels of an *am ha'aretz*. That is, in other vessels, there is a concern that they will be mixed with an "am ha'aretz," the earthiness and materiality of the world. This is not the case with food and liquids (which become blood and flesh like one's own flesh), because a *talmid chacham* does not use the food and liquids of an *am ha'aretz* at all.

One should add—by way of *drush* and hint—that even the reason according to the revealed Torah that they did not issue a decree regarding a person, "because it is an uncommon occurrence," hints at the inner reason mentioned above, for such a person whose *avodah* in refining and purifying their body is perfect, until they are at the level of an "earthenware vessel" that has a "tzamid patil upon it," is an "uncommon occurrence" in the (parameters of the) world.

E. Now, although there are two types of vessels that protect from *tumah* with a *tzamid patil* as mentioned (vessels that do not contract *tumah* and earthenware vessels), nevertheless, the Rambam emphasizes (based on the language of the *Sifri*), that the source of the law that a *tzamid patil* protects from *tumah* in the tent of the dead was stated initially regarding an earthenware vessel specifically. In his words: "By tradition, it was learned that the verse speaks only of an earthenware vessel," but from this, they learned a *kal v'chomer* for all vessels that do not contract *tumah*.

One must examine why this law is hinted at regarding an earthenware vessel specifically, and not that the verse should speak initially also regarding all vessels that do not contract *tumah*?

The explanation for this in the inner dimension of things is based on the above:
Although on the face of it, the level of the first way is greater, where a person elevates themselves above the category of *tumah* as mentioned, nevertheless, the ultimate intent of the creation of man and the descent of their soul into this world to be enclothed in a body is to refine the body and purify it. In the well-known expression: "For the soul itself does not need correction at all," and its descent into this world is only to correct the body, the animal soul, and one's portion in the world (as explained at length in the holy *Tanya*).

This is what the Rambam wrote, "that the verse speaks only of an earthenware vessel"—because the supreme intent (in which "the verse speaks") is that a person should work with their body and act upon it so that it will be at the level of an "earthenware vessel."

Based on this, we can explain the third way mentioned above (Section A-B)—regarding the law of "a chimney between a house and an attic, with *tumah* in the house and a pot placed over the opening of the chimney," where the Sages decreed *tumah* also on the person in the attic:

In this case, the person is not inside a "vessel" at all—not inside an earthenware vessel and not inside a vessel that does not contract *tumah*, but is in the attic. Its spiritual meaning is that a person thinks that in order to protect themselves from the *tumah* of this world, they elevate themselves to an "attic," separating themselves from the matters of the world, and their entire connection to the world is "from afar" (through a "chimney").

That is to say: Even regarding the way of being in a vessel that does not contract *tumah*, i.e., a person who serves Hashem with their soul (as mentioned), their *avodah* acts upon their body, but their action is from the side of the soul's light that shines within their body, until their body becomes *batel* due to the intensity of the soul's light and therefore is not considered a "vessel," as mentioned.

This is not the case for a person who is in an "attic," which means that their *avodah* is in complete separation from physical things, that they do not engage with their body at all to illuminate it with the light of their soul, thinking that through this they are guarded from the *tumah* of this world, etc.

To this comes the instruction of the Torah, that the opposite is true: Although by the letter of the law (according to the Torah), they are *tahor* because they are not in the tent of the dead ("the place of death" of this world), nevertheless, it is specifically in this way that the Sages decreed *tumah* on the person (which they did not decree in the two ways mentioned above). For the true state of this person who is in the "attic" is that they are not essentially separated from the *tumah* of this world. The fact that the *tumah* of this world does not affect them is only because they actually separate themselves from it, but from the side of their own existence, they are connected to the world of *tumah*, etc.

This is not the case in the first two ways, where the person does not separate from the matters of the world, but their *avodah* is within a "vessel"; there, the Sages did not decree *tumah* on the person, and they are *tahor*.

(From a *sicha* of 20 Cheshvan, 5745)


## פינחס

## Sicha 1

### 103

A. Regarding the verse, "To these the land shall be divided as an inheritance according to the number of names," the Sifri states: "Everything is included—Yisrael, Kohanim, and Leviim, etc. And Hashem said to Aharon, 'In their land you shall not inherit'—this excludes the Kohanim; 'They shall not inherit an inheritance'—this excludes the Leviim, etc."

This is brought as Halacha in the Rambam in the final chapter of Hilchos Shemitah V'Yovel: "The entire tribe of Levi is warned not to inherit in the land of Canaan. Similarly, they are warned not to take a portion of the spoils when they conquer the cities, as it is said, 'The Kohanim, the Leviim, the entire tribe of Levi, shall have no portion or inheritance with Yisrael'—a portion in the spoils and an inheritance in the land. And so it says, 'In their land you shall not inherit'—[this refers to] the land; [and] 'you shall have no portion'—[this refers to] the spoils."

Regarding this, the Mishneh Lamelech writes: The Sefer Mitzvos Gadol (Smag) writes that in the future to come, they will take a portion in the land, as it is said, "One gate for Levi," as is found in the chapter *Yesh Nochalin*. His intent is to the Beraisa there: "The land of Yisrael is destined to be divided into thirteen tribes, whereas initially it was divided only into twelve tribes." According to the commentary of the Rashbam: "And further on it explains what that additional portion is. For if it were because Levi is mentioned as taking an inheritance like the other tribes, as it is written there, 'One gate for Levi,' then Menashe and Ephraim also would only take one portion, as it is written there, 'One gate for Yosef.'... [This refers to the fact] that initially, when they came to the land, it was divided only into twelve tribes, for Levi had no portion in the land, but each tribe gave them cities to dwell in, etc." (unlike in the future, as mentioned above). This means that according to the Rashbam, the tribe of Levi will take one portion out of the twelve portions into which the land was divided (and the thirteenth portion is "for the Nasi"—"for Melech HaMashiach").

Now, the Shita Mekubetzes on Bava Basra there brings the words of the Ran, who questions the Rashbam from several verses in Yechezkel which clarify that Yosef will take two portions. "And likewise, what he said, that the tribe of Levi will take a portion in the land, this too is incorrect, for it is explicitly stated in the passage that he will not take a portion in the land, but rather they will have cities to dwell in, and these are from the portion of the *terumah* that they shall set aside, etc." He concludes: "Rather, the essence of the matter is that just as the tribe of Levi did not take

### 104

a portion in the first [conquest], so too they will not take in the second, except that they were given cities to dwell in near Yerushalayim, and they are part of the *terumas hakodesh* [holy offering]".

B. One must examine this regarding the opinion of the Rambam, who states simply: "The entire tribe of Levi is warned not to inherit in the land of Canaan." What is his position regarding the inheritance of the tribe of Levi in the land in the future to come?

In *Minchas Chinuch*, he writes that according to the Rambam, even in the future to come, the Leviim will not take an inheritance in the land. He proves this from the fact that the Rambam did not write this explicitly—just as he wrote explicitly in *Hilchos Melachim*, "Melech HaMashiach takes one-thirteenth of all the lands that Yisrael conquers".

However, since the Smag's "custom is always to follow in the footsteps of the Rambam, and in most places in his book he does nothing but copy [him], etc.", it is reasonable to say, on the face of it, that in this matter too, the Smag holds that this is the opinion of the Rambam [as is also proven from the plain language of the Smag, who first brings the words of the Rambam from the subsequent halacha that if a King of Yisrael conquers, etc., the Kohanim and Leviim take a portion and inheritance there, and afterwards adds, "And in the future to come, they will take a portion even in the land, as it is said, 'One gate for Levi'"]. But it requires study: whence does he know this?

Also, the very essence of this position requires study (as the commentators have questioned): since it is a negative commandment in the Torah that the tribe of Levi shall not take a portion in the land, how can the mitzvah be uprooted in the days of Melech HaMashiach? Are these not the mitzvos etc., that no prophet is permitted to innovate anything? On the other hand, since

### 105

the Rambam and likewise the Smag counted this negative commandment in the count of the mitzvos, and the Rambam already wrote in *Sefer Hamitzvos*, Root 3, "that it is not appropriate to count mitzvos that are not practiced for all generations"—if this mitzvah will be nullified in the division of the land in the future, what place is there to count it in the list of negative commandments?

[Regarding the words of the Sifri mentioned above—"To these the land shall be divided... everything is included—Yisrael, Kohanim, and Leviim, converts, etc. And Hashem said to Aharon, 'In their land you shall not inherit'—this excludes the Kohanim; 'They shall not inherit an inheritance'—this excludes the Leviim, etc."—there are those who explain that there is no mitzvah here at all, and the intent is only to negate their right to the inheritance of the land, to say that the Kohanim have no portion and inheritance in the land, similar to converts, women, and slaves. But there is no negative warning here against them not to inherit, just as there is no warning against converts, women, and slaves. The verse only excludes them to say that they have no right to a portion and inheritance in the land, but if it is given to them, they are permitted to receive it, and there is no prohibition in this, neither for the givers nor for the recipients (and seemingly this is implied by the plain meaning of the verses in our *parsha*, "To these the land shall be divided as an inheritance," and further on, "And these are the counts of the Leviim, etc., and their counts were, etc., they were not counted among the Bnei Yisrael because no inheritance was given to them among the Bnei Yisrael").

But according to the opinion of the Rambam and the Smag that the negative commandment is counted in the list of mitzvos, it is not understood—how can it be said that in the future the Leviim will take a portion in the land, when, since it will be nullified, it is not appropriate to count it in the list of negative commandments?]

C. It seems to me that the simple explanation lies in the precision of the Rambam's language, when he writes the warning, "The entire tribe of Levi is warned not to inherit in the land of Canaan," and not the usual language, "in the land of Yisrael" or "in the land," etc. It requires study: what does he exclude by this?

Seemingly, one could say that he excludes other lands,

### 106

for "all the lands that Yisrael conquers by a King with the consent of the Beis Din is a conquest of the many, and it is like the land of Yisrael that Yehoshua conquered in every respect." Even though this is included in the land of Yisrael, as the Rambam wrote, "The land of Yisrael mentioned everywhere is the lands that the King of Yisrael conquers, etc., with the consent of the majority of Yisrael," nevertheless, in these lands, they are not warned, "In their land you shall not inherit."

But it is forced to explain that this is all the Rambam intends to exclude, for the Rambam explicitly excludes this in the subsequent halacha: "It appears to me that these words apply only to the land regarding which a covenant was made with Avraham, Yitzchak, and Yaakov, and their children inherited it and it was divided among them. But all other lands that a King of the Kings of Yisrael conquers, the Kohanim and Leviim in those lands and in their spoils are like all of Yisrael."

Simply put, his intent with "land of Canaan" is to exclude land that is essentially part of the land of Yisrael but is not part of the land of Canaan, and that is the land of the Keini, Kenizi, and Kadmoni.

[As the Rambam wrote in *Hilchos Rotzeach* regarding the addition of cities of refuge in the days of Melech HaMashiach: "And where do they add them? In the cities of the Keini, Kenizi, and Kadmoni, regarding which a covenant was made with Avraham Avinu, and they have not yet been conquered, and regarding them it is said in the Torah, 'And if Hashem your G-d expands your border'"].

Based on this, it is understood that even according to the Rambam, it is possible to say and explain that which is said, "One gate for Levi," that in the future they will take a portion in the land, but

### 107

not in the land of Canaan, but in the land of the Keini, Kenizi, and Kadmoni.

— And the warning, "The Kohanim, the Leviim, the entire tribe of Levi, shall have no portion or inheritance with Yisrael," is a mitzvah that applies for all generations in the land of Canaan.

However, the Rambam did not write explicitly that in the future to come the Leviim will inherit in the land of the Keini, Kenizi, and Kadmoni (except by way of a hint only, in the precision of his language that they shall not inherit "in the land of Canaan"), according to the known rules of the Rambam that it is not his way to write novel matters, and he does nothing but copy the Gemara (whereas "a matter that did not come in the Gemara except by way of a question, incidentally, is not their way to copy").

D. But it requires explanation:
Regarding the reason why the tribe of Levi did not take a portion in the land, the Rambam wrote: "And why did Levi not merit the inheritance of the land of Yisrael and its spoils with his brothers? Because he was set apart to serve Hashem, to minister to Him, and to teach His righteous ways and His just judgments to the many, as it is said, 'They shall teach Your judgments to Yaakov and Your Torah to Yisrael.' Therefore, they were set apart from the ways of the world; they do not engage in war like the rest of Yisrael, nor do they inherit, etc. Rather, they are the army of Hashem, as it is said, 'Bless, Hashem, his army,' and He, blessed be He, merits them, as it is said, 'I am your portion and your inheritance.'"

Based on this, it is not understood: why, in the land of the Keini, Kenizi, and Kadmoni, which are destined to be an inheritance in the future, will the Leviim take a portion?

### 108

One could say the explanation for this is:
The warning that they shall not inherit in the land, "In their land you shall not inherit," means in the land of Yisrael (the land of Canaan) which was given to them so that they would have a land to dwell in (similar to how other nations have a land and inheritance in the world). Regarding this, the tribe of Levi was warned, "The Kohanim, the Leviim, etc., shall have no portion or inheritance with Yisrael," for a portion and inheritance in the land in this manner is in the category of "the ways of the world," and the tribe of Levi, "because he was set apart to serve Hashem," this portion and inheritance does not belong to them, for "they were set apart from the ways of the world."

But that which Yisrael will receive in the future to come, the land of the Keini, Kenizi, and Kadmoni, is not in the category of "inheritance" like the land of Canaan, the land of the seven nations, which Yisrael has as a land in the world, but rather a special gift from Hashem. And as it is said, "And if Hashem your G-d expands your border, etc."—not from the necessity of their settlement in the land. And if so, the Leviim can certainly receive [a portion] in this.

E. In the inner dimension of these matters, one could say:
The difference between the land of Canaan—the seven nations—and the land of the Keini, Kenizi, and Kadmoni—the land of the three nations—is explained in many places, that in the service of Hashem, this is the difference between the refinement of the seven *midos* and the service in the three *mochin*. Currently, the essence of the service is in the refinement of the seven *midos*, and in the future, the three *mochin* will also be refined.

The difference in the manner of service is analogous to the difference between *midos* and *mochin*. *Midos* relate to the other; the matter of *midos* is the person's relating with emotion and *hispaalus* to something outside of himself, and it is a descent for the person himself. The matter of *mochin* is for oneself, as we see that people who engage in intellectual matters seclude themselves for themselves. Such is the difference in the manner of their refinement: the refinement of the *midos* is through conquest and war, which is through *hislabshus* (enclothement) and refinement, whereas the refinement of the *mochin* is not primarily through *hislabshus* in the garments of that which is being refined, but on the contrary, through separation and abstraction.

This is the difference between the "land of Canaan," the land of the seven nations, and the "Keini, Kenizi, and Kadmoni." The land of Canaan represents the service of conquest and refinement of the seven *midos*, and therefore the tribe of Levi, because he was set apart to serve Hashem—which is above conquest and above refinements—does not belong to the inheritance

### 109

in the land of Canaan. Whereas the land of the Keini, Kenizi, and Kadmoni, which is the service in the *mochin* that are above refinements and conquest—therefore it belongs (also) to the tribe of Levi.

F. And it still requires understanding:
Regarding the time of the future to come, the Rambam wrote at the end of *Hilchos Melachim* that "the entire world will be occupied only with knowing Hashem alone." It turns out that in the future to come, all of Yisrael will be set apart from the ways of the world, for their entire occupation will be only in knowing Hashem (similar to what the Rambam wrote at the end of *Hilchos Shemitah V'Yovel* that "not only the tribe of Levi, but every single person, etc., whose spirit moved him and whose knowledge understood him to set himself apart to stand before Hashem to serve Him and to worship Him, to know Hashem, etc., he is sanctified as holy of holies, and Hashem will be his portion and his inheritance forever, etc., just as He merited the Kohanim and the Leviim, etc."). If so, why will there be this difference then between Yisrael and Leviim, that all of Yisrael will have a portion and inheritance in the land of Canaan—the matter of refining the *midos* and enclothement in the garments of that which is being refined—unlike the tribe of Levi?

The explanation for this:
